Refuting the Opponent's Claim that the Masdar is the True Object
The author refutes the opponent's conflation of masdar with object and highlights his self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's claim that the verbal noun (masdar) is the true object, and exposes the opponent's self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Grammatical categories like subject and object are conventional, not real.

The opponent tries to equate the verbal noun (masdar) with the theological 'object', but the author shows that even linguists disagree on this classification, and that grammatical roles do not reflect real occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the masdar and event-nouns
Read from 'Then he called the verbal nouns events of the nouns' through the example of hitting, eating, drinking.
Notice the opponent's claim that the masdar is the object in reality.
2
Understand the linguistic disagreement
The author points out that the Basrans say the masdar is an object, but al-Farra' (a leading grammarian) says it is a verb. This internal disagreement undermines the opponent's reliance on a single view.
Al-Farra's position shows the matter is not settled.
3
Examine the grammatical examples
The author gives examples like 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr' and 'Khalid died' to show that grammatical subject and object do not correspond to real agent and patient.
This is the core proof that grammar does not reflect reality.
4
Identify the self-contradiction
The author shows that the opponent first states that grammarians distinguish between verb and object, then later says nothing was taken from them on this distinction.
This contradiction makes his argument invalid.

يعدون ذلك فعلا بل يعدونه اسما، ولهذا اختص بعلامات الأسماء وهي دخول لام التعريف أو التنوين أو الإضافة دون علامات الأفعال وهي قد وسوف، ودخل فيما هو من حد الاسم عندهم، وهو ما دل على معنى غير مقترن بزمان محصل أو كلمة تدل على المعنى دلالة الإشارة دون الإفادة، وخرج من حد الأفعال عندهم وهو ما دل على خلاف، وما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام ليس بفعل عند أهل اللغة. فكيف تلزم خصومك الأخذ بقولهم. ثم إنه سمى المصادر أحداث الأسماء، فكان ضرب زيد وأكل خالد وشرب محمد أحداثا لهؤلاء، أتقول أنت: إن هذه المعاني حدثت عنهم؟ فإن قلت : نعم، فقد جعلت للعباد أحداثا وإن قلت: لا، فقد خالفت أهل اللغة . ثم قوله في آخر هذا الفصل : إن عندهم المفعول في الحقيقة هو المصدر، ثم يقولون مفعول به و مفعول له ومفعول فيه ومفعول معه ولا يزيدون على ذلك، وما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول من أوله إلى آخره كلام فاسد متناقض وقع فيه لجهله بمواصفات أهل اللغة ونبين ذلك فنقول : قوله إن عندهم المفعول في الحقيقة هو المصدر، هذا شيء يقوله البصريون، وخالفهم فيه الفراء، فقال: إن المصدر ليس بمفعول بل هو فعل، والمفعول ما تعلق به الفعل لا نفس الفعل فلم يكن هذا مقبولا من البصريين مع مخالفة الفراء إياهم وهو من رؤساء أهل اللغة، ثم نقول : المفعول عندهم ليس ما هو المفعول حقيقة ، وكذا الفاعل وكذا الفعل. بل الفعل عندهم ما مر والفاعل عندهم ما أسند إليه ما هو الفعل عندهم وهو المحدث عنه لا من فعل حقيقة، والمفعول ما لم يسند إليه ما هو الفعل عندهم وخرج فاعلا من أن يكون محدثا عنه، فإنك إذا قلت: لم يضرب زيد عمرا، كان زيد عندهم وعمرو مفعولا ولو قلت مات خالد وطال الغلام وأسود الشعر وأبيض الثلج، كان ارتفاع كل اسم من ذلك لكونه فاعلا ، ولا شك أن الفعل لم يوجد من أحد هؤلاء، ولكنه لما حدث عنه وأسند إليه ما هو الفعل في صناعتهم عد فاعلا. وما عندهم من خرج عن الحديث ولم يسند إليه كان مفعولا وإن لم يقع عليه فعل ما كان المصدر عند البصريين مفعولا مطلقا لأن ما هو الفعل لم يسند إليه ولم يحدث عنه فكان عندهم على مقتضى صناعتهم مفعولا لا أن يكون مفعولا حقيقة كما لو قلت: عبدت الله تعالى كان انتصاب قولك : الله ، لكونه مفعولا، وكذا لو قلت: لم يعبد فلان الله وليست حقيقة الفعل واقعة على الله تعالى - تعالى الله عن ذلك - فإذا ما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام كان مفعولا عند البصريين من أهل اللغة، فأما ما هو الفعل عند أهل اللغة فليس بمفعول عندهم، وأما ما هو مفعول عندهم ليس بفعل البتة. وشيء من هذا لا يتصل بما نحن فيه، وبهذا يبطل قوله: ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فإن أهل اللغة جعلوا الفعل الأمثلة التي بينا وهي مأخوذة عندهم من المصدر وهو المفعول المطلق عندهم ، وهو غير تلك الأمثلة المأخوذة عنه، فكيف يستقيم قوله : ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فصار مؤخر كلامه مناقضا أول كلامه، فإنه حكى عنهم في أول كلامه الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، ثم قال في آخر كلامه ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الأمرين، وهذا هو التناقض الظاهر، وإنما ثبت الاتحاد بين ما هو حقيقة الفعل عند أهل الكلام وهو المصدر لا عندهم وبين ما هو مفعول في صناعتهم دون أهل الكلام، وما هو المفعول عندهم ليس بمفعول حقيقة عند أهل الكلام، فإذا لم يثبت الاتحاد بهذا بين ما
They count that as a verb, rather they count it as a noun, and for this it is distinguished by the markers of nouns, namely the entry of the definite article (lam al-ta'rif), or nunation (tanwin), or construct state (idafa), without the markers of verbs which are 'qad' and 'sawfa', and it entered into what is from the definition of the noun according to them, which is that which indicates a meaning not coupled with a determined time, or a word indicating a meaning by reference (dalalat al-ishara) without predication (ifada), and it exited from the definition of verbs according to them, which is that which indicates something else. And what is the act (al-fi'l) according to the people of kalam is not an act according to the people of language. So how can you oblige your opponents to accept their statement? Then he called the verbal nouns (masadir) 'events of the nouns' (ahdath al-asma'), so 'Zayd's hitting', 'Khalid's eating', and 'Muhammad's drinking' became events belonging to them. Do you say that these meanings occurred from them? If you say yes, then you have made events for the servants. If you say no, then you have contradicted the people of language. Then his statement at the end of this chapter: 'Verily, according to them the object (al-maf'ul) in reality is the verbal noun (al-masdar).' Then they say: 'object with the verb' (maf'ul bihi), 'object of purpose' (maf'ul lahu), 'object of place/time' (maf'ul fihi), and 'object of accompaniment' (maf'ul ma'ahu) and they do not exceed that. And his saying: 'What has been taken from them of the distinction between the verb and the object, from its beginning to its end, is corrupt, contradictory speech in which he fell due to his ignorance of the specifications of the people of language, and we will explain that.' So we say: His statement that according to them the object in reality is the verbal noun — this is something the Basrans say, and al-Farra' disagreed with them in it, saying: 'The verbal noun is not an object; rather it is a verb, and the object is that which the verb is attached to, not the verb itself.' So this [Basran position] is not accepted because of al-Farra's opposition to them, and he is one of the leaders of the people of language. Then we say: The object according to them is not what is the object in reality; likewise the subject (al-fa'il) and likewise the verb. Rather, the verb according to them is what has passed, and the subject according to them is that to which is attributed what is the verb according to them, which is the spoken-of thing (al-muhaddath 'anhu), not the one who truly acted. And the object is that to which what is the verb according to them is not attributed, and it emerges as a subject from being spoken-of. For if you say: 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr', Zayd according to them and 'Amr are both objects. And if you say: 'Khalid died', 'the boy grew tall', 'the hair turned black', and 'the snow turned white', the elevated status of each noun in that is because it is a subject, and there is no doubt that the verb did not occur from any one of them, but because it was spoken-of and attributed to it what is the verb in their craft, it is counted as a subject. And what according to them exits from being spoken-of and is not attributed to it is an object, even if no verb occurs upon it. The verbal noun according to the Basrans was an absolute object (maf'ul mutlaq) because what is the verb was not attributed to it and it was not spoken-of about it, so it became according to them, by the requirement of their craft, an object, not that it is an object in reality — as if you say: 'I worshipped God Almighty,' the vertical form (intisab) of your saying 'God' is because it is an object, and likewise if you say: 'So-and-so did not worship God,' and the reality of the verb does not occur upon God Almighty — exalted is God above that. So when what is the verb according to the people of kalam was an object according to the Basran people of language — as for what is the verb according to the people of language, it is not an object according to them, and as for what is an object according to them, it is not a verb at all. And none of this relates to what we are in, and by this his statement is invalidated: 'What has been taken from them of the distinction between the verb and the object.' For the people of language made the verb to be the paradigms (amthila) which we mentioned, and they are taken according to them from the verbal noun, which is the absolute object according to them, and it is other than those paradigms taken from it. So how can his statement 'what has been taken from them of the distinction between the verb and the object' be sound? So the later part of his speech became contradictory to the beginning of his speech, for he narrated from them at the beginning of his speech the distinction between the verb and the object, then he said at the end of his speech what has been taken from them of the distinction between the two matters, and this is clear contradiction. And only unity is established between what is the reality of the verb according to the people of kalam — which is the verbal noun, not according to them — and between what is an object in their craft, not according to the people of kalam. And what is the object according to them is not an object in reality according to the people of kalam. So when the unity is not established by this between what
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who conflates linguistic (grammatical) and theological definitions of 'act' (fi'l) and 'object/patient' (maf'ul). The author identifies the opponent's mistake and demonstrates its self-contradiction.

The passage begins by noting that what theologians call an act (fi'l) is actually classified as a noun (ism) by grammarians, because it takes the markers of nouns (definite article, nunation, construct state) and not the markers of verbs (qad, sawfa). Thus, the theological definition of 'act' differs completely from the grammatical one. The opponent, however, tries to force his theological opponents to accept linguistic categories, which is fallacious.

The author then cites the opponent's claim that the 'true object' (al-maf'ul fi al-haqiqa) is the verbal noun (masdar). The author notes that this is a Basran grammatical position, but it was opposed by al-Farra', a leading Kufan grammarian, who argued that the masdar is a verb, not an object. So even within grammar, the matter is not settled.

Next, the author explains that grammarians do not define 'subject' (fa'il), 'object' (maf'ul), and 'verb' (fi'l) according to real-world action and reception. Instead, the 'subject' is simply the word to which the 'verb' (in their conventional sense) is attributed, regardless of whether any real action occurred. Examples illustrate this: in 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr', both Zayd and 'Amr are called 'objects' grammatically, even though the hitting did not happen. In 'Khalid died', 'the boy grew tall', etc., the nouns are called 'subjects' (fa'il) even though no action issued from them. Thus, grammatical categories do not reflect theological reality.

The author then applies this to the opponent's claim: since the masdar is not attributed to any subject in the grammatical sense, it is conventionally called an 'object' (maf'ul mutlaq) by Basrans. But this does not make it a true object in theological terms. The author gives the example of 'I worshipped God': even if one says 'So-and-so did not worship God', the word 'God' is still grammatically an object, yet no act actually occurs upon God. So the theological 'act' (what really occurs) is grammatically an 'object', not an 'act'. This shows that the opponent's reliance on grammatical distinctions is invalid.

Finally, the author points out the opponent's self-contradiction: he first claimed that grammarians distinguish between verb and object, but then claimed that nothing was taken from them on this distinction. His own words are inconsistent. The attempted unity between the grammatical object and the theological act fails, because the grammatical object is not a real object in theology. Thus, the opponent's argument is refuted.

صناعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verbal noun (masdar) is the true object in reality.
Correction: The passage shows that even linguists disagree, and grammatical object does not imply real occurrence.

Checkpoint
What contradictory statements did the opponent make?
A) He first claimed grammarians distinguish between verb and object, then said nothing was taken from them on this distinction.
B) He first agreed with Basrans then disagreed.
C) He said the masdar is a verb then an object.
D) He cited both Basran and Kufan views.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument collapses because grammatical categories are conventional and his own statements contradict each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent was caught in a clear self-contradiction. When have you held two contradictory beliefs without realizing it? What would it take for you to honestly examine your own assumptions and admit inconsistency?
?
This lesson shows that words like 'subject' and 'object' are conventional labels, not fixed realities. Is there a term or concept in your faith that you have mistakenly treated as absolute, when it is actually a human convention? How would you find out?
?
The author uses precise linguistic analysis to defend theological truth. How much effort do you invest in learning the exact wording and arguments of your creed? Could your laziness in this area leave you vulnerable to the same kind of contradiction this opponent fell into?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Grammatical Distinction of فاعل and مفعول Does Not Imply Agency
Nasafi explains grammatical فاعل and مفعول with examples and contrasts them with reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the grammatical categories of فاعل (subject) and مفعول (object) in Arabic do not correspond to actual agency or patiency in reality.

Core Idea
Grammatical subject (فاعل) does not mean actual doer; it's a linguistic role.

Imam Nasafi begins by examining how Arabic grammarians use the terms فاعل and مفعول. Through examples like 'Zayd did not hit Amr' and stative verbs, he shows that these labels are based on syntactic structure, not on whether an action truly originated from the subject. This sets the stage for refuting an opponent who tries to equate linguistic categories with theological ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi uses examples to make his point.
2
Identify the examples
Find the examples: negated action sentence (Zayd did not hit Amr), stative verbs (died, grew tall, turned black/white), and the verbal noun (مصدر) as absolute object.
These show that grammatical labeling is not tied to real agency.
3
Contrast linguistic and theological terms
Note Nasafi's key distinction: what theologians call 'action' (فعل) is called 'object' (مفعول) by Basran grammarians, and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أن يكون محدثا عنه، فإنك إذا قلت: لم يضرب زيد عمرا، كان زيد عندهم وعمرو مفعولا ولو قلت مات خالد وطال الغلام وأسود الشعر وأبيض الثلج، كان ارتفاع كل اسم من ذلك لكونه فاعلا، ولا شك أن الفعل لم يوجد من أحد هؤلاء، ولكنه لما حدث عنه وأسند إليه ما هو الفعل في صناعتهم عد فاعلا. وما عندهم من خرج عن الحديث ولم يسند إليه كان مفعولا وإن لم يقع عليه فعل ما كان المصدر عند البصريين مفعولا مطلقا لأن ما هو الفعل لم يسند إليه ولم يحدث عنه فكان عندهم على مقتضى صناعتهم مفعولا لا أن يكون مفعولا حقيقة كما لو قلت: عبدت الله تعالى كان انتصاب قولك: الله، لكونه مفعولا، وكذا لو قلت: لم يعبد فلان الله وليست حقيقة الفعل واقعة على الله تعالى - تعالى الله عن ذلك - فإذا ما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام كان مفعولا عند البصريين من أهل اللغة، فأما ما هو الفعل عند أهل اللغة فليس بمفعول عندهم، وأما ما هو مفعول عندهم ليس بفعل البتة. وشيء من هذا لا يتصل بما نحن فيه، وبهذا يبطل قوله: ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فإن أهل اللغة جعلوا الفعل الأمثلة التي بينا وهي مأخوذة عندهم من المصدر وهو المفعول المطلق عندهم، وهو غير تلك الأمثلة المأخوذة عنه، فكيف يستقيم قوله: ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فصار مؤخر كلامه مناقضا أول كلامه، فإنه حكى عنهم في أول كلامه الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، ثم قال في آخر كلامه ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الأمرين، وهذا هو التناقض الظاهر، وإنما ثبت الاتحاد بين ما هو حقيقة الفعل عند أهل الكلام وهو المصدر لا عندهم وبين ما هو مفعول في صناعتهم دون أهل الكلام، وما هو المفعول عندهم ليس بمفعول حقيقة عند أهل الكلام، فإذا لم يثبت الاتحاد بهذا بين ما هو الفعل والمفعول عند أهل اللغة، ولا الاتحاد بين ما هو الفعل والمفعول عند أهل الكلام. فكان التعلق بهذا تلبيسا ظاهرا، وبالله العصمة.
From being that which is related about, for if you say: 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr,' then Zayd according to them is doer and 'Amr is object, and if you say: 'Khalid died' and 'the boy grew tall' and 'the hair turned black' and 'the snow turned white,' the nominative case of each noun in that is because it is a doer, and there is no doubt that the action did not originate from any of these, but because it was related about and attributed to what is the action in their technical usage, it is counted as a doer. And what according to them departed from relation and was not attributed to it, it was object, even if no action actually occurred to it. The verbal noun according to the Basrans is an absolute object because what is the action is not attributed to it and is not related about it, so it is according to them by the requirement of their technical usage an object, not that it is an object in reality, just as if you say: 'I worshiped Allah Most High,' the accusative of your saying 'Allah' is because it is an object, and likewise if you say: 'So-and-so did not worship Allah' and the reality of the action does not occur upon Allah Most High — exalted is Allah above that. So what is the action according to the people of kalam is an object according to the Basrans among the linguists; as for what is the action according to the linguists, it is not an object according to them, and as for what is object according to them, it is not an action at all. And none of this pertains to our subject, and by this his statement is invalidated: 'They took the distinction between action and object.' For the linguists made the action the patterns which we mentioned, and they are taken by them from the verbal noun, which is the absolute object according to them, and it is other than those patterns taken from it. So how can his statement 'They took the distinction between action and object' be sound? Thus the latter part of his speech contradicts the first part of his speech, for he reported from them at the beginning of his speech the distinction between action and object, then he said at the end of his speech 'They did not take the distinction between the two matters,' and this is clear contradiction. And only the identity is established between what is the reality of action according to the people of kalam — which is the verbal noun, not according to them — and between what is object in their technical usage without the people of kalam, and what is object according to them is not an object in reality according to the people of kalam. So if identity is not established by this between what is action and object according to the linguists, nor identity between what is action and object according to the people of kalam, then adherence to this is manifest obfuscation, and with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument that relied on the linguistic distinction between 'doer' (فاعل) and 'object' (مفعول). Nasafi begins by explaining the grammatical treatment in Arabic: in sentences like 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr,' Zayd is considered the doer (فاعل) and 'Amr the object (مفعول) based on the grammatical structure, not on whether an action actually originated from them. He gives examples of stative verbs (died, grew tall, turned black/white) where the subject is called فاعل even though no action issues from it; this shows the grammatical category does not match theological reality. Similarly, the verbal noun (مصدر) is treated as an absolute object (مفعول مطلق) by Basran grammarians, not because it receives an action but due to grammatical convention. Nasafi then contrasts the terminology of linguists (أهل اللغة) and theologians (أهل الكلام): what theologians call 'action' (فعل) — meaning the event itself — is called 'object' by Basran grammarians, while what linguists call 'action' (the verb form) is not an object for them, and what they call 'object' is never an action. Therefore, the opponent's claim that theologians 'took the distinction between action and object from the linguists' is false; the two disciplines use the terms differently. Nasafi points out internal contradiction in the opponent's own words: he first says the linguists distinguish between action and object, then says they did not take that distinction from them. This refutes the opponent's linguistic argument. He concludes that no identity exists between the categories of the two disciplines, and relying on such confusion is mere obfuscation.

Key Terms
فاعل
مفعول
مصدر
مفعول مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the grammatical فاعل always implies an actual doer or agent in reality.
Correction: Nasafi shows with stative verbs (died, grew tall) that no action originates from the subject, yet they are still called فاعل due to syntactic structure.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's example, why is 'Khalid' called فاعل in the sentence 'Khalid died' (مات خالد)?
A) Because Khalid performed the action of dying.
B) Because the verb 'died' is attributed to Khalid and he is the subject of the sentence.
C) Because Khalid caused his own death.
D) Because the verb is in the past tense.

Key Takeaway
The grammatical labels فاعل and مفعول are based on syntactic relations, not on the reality of action or reception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call yourself a 'believer' (مؤمن) — that is a grammatical label. But does your daily life produce actions that truly originate from that label, or are you just playing a syntactic role? What one action today can you perform that is genuinely from you, not just a label?
?
Think of a time you judged someone by the title they hold (e.g., 'teacher', 'parent', 'leader'). Did you assume their actions automatically matched that title? Based on this lesson, how can you separate the linguistic role from the reality of their deeds starting tomorrow?
?
When you describe your own failures, do you use passive language like 'it happened' or 'I was tested'? Are you hiding from personal responsibility behind grammatical structures? What is one mistake you can openly say 'I did' instead of 'it happened to me'?

The Invalidity of Using Linguistic Distinctions in Theological Arguments
Nasafi refutes the opponent's linguistic argument and shows its contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's argument that equates linguistic and theological categories of فاعل and مفعول is invalid, as shown by Nasafi's refutation.

Core Idea
Linguistic and theological categories of subject and object are incommensurable.

Having established that grammatical فاعل and مفعول do not correspond to actual agency, Nasafi now directly refutes the opponent's claim that the distinction between action and object was taken from the linguists. He points out a contradiction in the opponent's own speech and shows that none of the linguistic categories apply to the theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Nasafi declares the linguistic discussion irrelevant and then invalidates the opponent's claim.
2
Identify the contradiction
Nasafi notes that the opponent first says linguists distinguish between action and object, then later says they did not take that distinction. This is a clear contradiction.
نقض ظاهر
3
Understand the failure of identity
Nasafi explains that the identity claimed between linguistic and theological categories is not established; the opponent's argument is mere obfuscation.
تلبيس

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أن يكون محدثا عنه، فإنك إذا قلت: لم يضرب زيد عمرا، كان زيد عندهم وعمرو مفعولا ولو قلت مات خالد وطال الغلام وأسود الشعر وأبيض الثلج، كان ارتفاع كل اسم من ذلك لكونه فاعلا، ولا شك أن الفعل لم يوجد من أحد هؤلاء، ولكنه لما حدث عنه وأسند إليه ما هو الفعل في صناعتهم عد فاعلا. وما عندهم من خرج عن الحديث ولم يسند إليه كان مفعولا وإن لم يقع عليه فعل ما كان المصدر عند البصريين مفعولا مطلقا لأن ما هو الفعل لم يسند إليه ولم يحدث عنه فكان عندهم على مقتضى صناعتهم مفعولا لا أن يكون مفعولا حقيقة كما لو قلت: عبدت الله تعالى كان انتصاب قولك: الله، لكونه مفعولا، وكذا لو قلت: لم يعبد فلان الله وليست حقيقة الفعل واقعة على الله تعالى - تعالى الله عن ذلك - فإذا ما هو الفعل عند أهل الكلام كان مفعولا عند البصريين من أهل اللغة، فأما ما هو الفعل عند أهل اللغة فليس بمفعول عندهم، وأما ما هو مفعول عندهم ليس بفعل البتة. وشيء من هذا لا يتصل بما نحن فيه، وبهذا يبطل قوله: ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فإن أهل اللغة جعلوا الفعل الأمثلة التي بينا وهي مأخوذة عندهم من المصدر وهو المفعول المطلق عندهم، وهو غير تلك الأمثلة المأخوذة عنه، فكيف يستقيم قوله: ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، فصار مؤخر كلامه مناقضا أول كلامه، فإنه حكى عنهم في أول كلامه الفصل بين الفعل والمفعول، ثم قال في آخر كلامه ما أخذ منهم الفصل بين الأمرين، وهذا هو التناقض الظاهر، وإنما ثبت الاتحاد بين ما هو حقيقة الفعل عند أهل الكلام وهو المصدر لا عندهم وبين ما هو مفعول في صناعتهم دون أهل الكلام، وما هو المفعول عندهم ليس بمفعول حقيقة عند أهل الكلام، فإذا لم يثبت الاتحاد بهذا بين ما هو الفعل والمفعول عند أهل اللغة، ولا الاتحاد بين ما هو الفعل والمفعول عند أهل الكلام. فكان التعلق بهذا تلبيسا ظاهرا، وبالله العصمة.
From being that which is related about, for if you say: 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr,' then Zayd according to them is doer and 'Amr is object, and if you say: 'Khalid died' and 'the boy grew tall' and 'the hair turned black' and 'the snow turned white,' the nominative case of each noun in that is because it is a doer, and there is no doubt that the action did not originate from any of these, but because it was related about and attributed to what is the action in their technical usage, it is counted as a doer. And what according to them departed from relation and was not attributed to it, it was object, even if no action actually occurred to it. The verbal noun according to the Basrans is an absolute object because what is the action is not attributed to it and is not related about it, so it is according to them by the requirement of their technical usage an object, not that it is an object in reality, just as if you say: 'I worshiped Allah Most High,' the accusative of your saying 'Allah' is because it is an object, and likewise if you say: 'So-and-so did not worship Allah' and the reality of the action does not occur upon Allah Most High — exalted is Allah above that. So what is the action according to the people of kalam is an object according to the Basrans among the linguists; as for what is the action according to the linguists, it is not an object according to them, and as for what is object according to them, it is not an action at all. And none of this pertains to our subject, and by this his statement is invalidated: 'They took the distinction between action and object.' For the linguists made the action the patterns which we mentioned, and they are taken by them from the verbal noun, which is the absolute object according to them, and it is other than those patterns taken from it. So how can his statement 'They took the distinction between action and object' be sound? Thus the latter part of his speech contradicts the first part of his speech, for he reported from them at the beginning of his speech the distinction between action and object, then he said at the end of his speech 'They did not take the distinction between the two matters,' and this is clear contradiction. And only the identity is established between what is the reality of action according to the people of kalam — which is the verbal noun, not according to them — and between what is object in their technical usage without the people of kalam, and what is object according to them is not an object in reality according to the people of kalam. So if identity is not established by this between what is action and object according to the linguists, nor identity between what is action and object according to the people of kalam, then adherence to this is manifest obfuscation, and with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument that relied on the linguistic distinction between 'doer' (فاعل) and 'object' (مفعول). Nasafi begins by explaining the grammatical treatment in Arabic: in sentences like 'Zayd did not hit 'Amr,' Zayd is considered the doer (فاعل) and 'Amr the object (مفعول) based on the grammatical structure, not on whether an action actually originated from them. He gives examples of stative verbs (died, grew tall, turned black/white) where the subject is called فاعل even though no action issues from it; this shows the grammatical category does not match theological reality. Similarly, the verbal noun (مصدر) is treated as an absolute object (مفعول مطلق) by Basran grammarians, not because it receives an action but due to grammatical convention. Nasafi then contrasts the terminology of linguists (أهل اللغة) and theologians (أهل الكلام): what theologians call 'action' (فعل) — meaning the event itself — is called 'object' by Basran grammarians, while what linguists call 'action' (the verb form) is not an object for them, and what they call 'object' is never an action. Therefore, the opponent's claim that theologians 'took the distinction between action and object from the linguists' is false; the two disciplines use the terms differently. Nasafi points out internal contradiction in the opponent's own words: he first says the linguists distinguish between action and object, then says they did not take that distinction from them. This refutes the opponent's linguistic argument. He concludes that no identity exists between the categories of the two disciplines, and relying on such confusion is mere obfuscation.

Key Terms
أهل اللغة
أهل الكلام
فاعل
مفعول
مصدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's argument that linguistic distinctions can be directly applied to theological concepts is coherent.
Correction: Nasafi shows it is self-contradictory and based on category confusion; the two fields use the same words differently.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi expose the opponent's contradiction?
A) By showing that the opponent's examples are grammatically incorrect.
B) By noting that the opponent first says linguists distinguish action and object, then later says they did not take that distinction.
C) By arguing that linguists do not use the terms فاعل and مفعول at all.
D) By claiming that the opponent invented the distinction himself.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument fails because it confuses linguistic and theological categories and contains internal contradiction; reliance on such obfuscation is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever used a word or concept in one context that you took from another, assuming it meant the same thing? What was the result? This passage shows the danger of such assumptions. How will you now approach key terms in your own understanding of faith?

The opponent's argument had an internal contradiction that he himself did not see. Are there areas in your beliefs or practices where you hold two conflicting ideas without realizing it? What step will you take this week to uncover and resolve such a contradiction?
?
Nasafi ends with 'with Allah is protection.' Do you seek Allah's protection from intellectual confusion and obfuscation? In what practical ways can you ensure that your religious knowledge is built on sound reasoning and clarity, not just convenient arguments?

The Objection and Refutation from the Objector's Own Principles
Nasafi presents an objection and refutes it using the objector's own statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes an analogy by pointing out the objector's own inconsistency regarding divine attributes and inherence.

Core Idea
Inherence implies spatial settling, impossible for God; the objector himself said so.

This passage introduces an objection and its immediate refutation. The objector draws an analogy between the union of state and inherence and the union of act and object. Nasafi's first move is to remind the objector that he himself denied that divine attributes inhere, thus his analogy collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's argument: since hal and hulul are considered united, then fi'l and maf'ul must also be considered united. Notice the structure of the analogy.
The objector assumes that hal and hulul are the same thing.
2
Identify Nasafi's first answer
Nasafi responds by quoting the objector's own words from his book on attributes. There, the objector argued that God's knowledge does not inhere in Him, and that inherence (hulul) means spatial settling, which cannot apply to God's attributes.
Nasafi uses the objector's own authority against him.
3
Understand the key distinction
Nasafi distinguishes between inherence (hulul) as physical settling and subsistence (qiyam) as a non-spatial relation. This shows that hal and hulul are not identical, breaking the analogy.
The distinction is crucial for understanding kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حال (1) بحلول هو نفسه ولم يلزم القول بأن الحلول غير الحال، فلما جاز القول باتحاد الحال والحلول جاز القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول ؟ والجواب عنه: أن يقال له، إنك أنت الذي ذكرت في مسألة الصفات في كتابك أن علم الباري لا يحله وليس هو محلا لعلمه، ولا لسائر صفات ذاته وإن كانت موجودة بذاته، لأن الحلول في اللغة هو السكون يقال حل فلان وارتحل، والعلم غير ساكن في العالم بل يستحيل أن توصف الصفة بالسكون والحركة ، ثم زعمت وكذلك الحركة غير حالة في المتحرك لما ذكرنا غير أنه يستعمل في وصف غيره ألفاظ المجاز، فأما في أوصاف الباري جل وعلا فيراعى التوقيف والحقيقة. ثم ذكرت الخلاف بين أصحاب الصفات (2) أن عبد الله بن سعيد (3) وأبا العباس القلانسي وغيرهما من متقدمي أصحاب الصفات كانوا يقولون: إن علم الباري قائم به، والأشعري اختار عبارة أخرى، وهي أن صفات الباري موجودة بذات الباري، وذكر هذا كله غيرك من الأشعريين في كتبهم منهم أبو منصور بن أيوب (4) ذكر في كتابه المترجم بالمقنع وهو الذي سلخته سلخا بل انتحلت أكثره بعبارته ونظمه وترتيبه ونسبت التصنيف إلى نفسك ولبسته على الضعفة من أصحابك وزوجته باسمك، ذكر هذه الفصول في ذلك الكتاب باللفظ الذي ذكرته، وكذا غير كما () من الأشعرية، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، هل يكون هذا الإلزام منك باتحاد الحال والحلول إلا غفلة راكدة وتحكما ظاهرا ووقاحة ظاهرة ووقاحة بينة دفعتك إليها الحيرة في نحلتك. ثم نقول : لو كنا أبينا عليك القول (2) باتحاد الفاعل والفعل فأوردت هذا لكان يوجب نوع خيال، فأما إذا كنا أبينا عليك (1) القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول ثم اشتغلت بإيراد مثل هذا النظير، كان ذلك منك جهلا بمواضع الإلزام والالتزام، وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
(Someone) said: 'If the state is the same as inherence and it is not necessary to say that inherence is other than the state, then when it is permissible to say that state and inherence are united, it becomes permissible to say that act and object of act are united?' And the answer to him: that it be said to him: 'You yourself mentioned in the issue of attributes in your book that the knowledge of the Exalted does not inhere in Him nor is He a locus for His knowledge, nor for the rest of His essential attributes, even though they exist through His essence, because inherence in language is settling – one says "so-and-so settled and departed" – and knowledge is not settling in the knower, rather it is impossible to describe the attribute with settling and motion. Then you claimed similarly that motion does not inhere in the moving thing for what we mentioned, except that figurative expressions are used in describing other than Him. As for the descriptions of the Exalted and Majestic, then restriction to scriptural usage and literal meaning is observed. Then you mentioned the disagreement among the proponents of attributes: that Abdullah ibn Sa'id and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and others from the early proponents of attributes used to say that the knowledge of the Exalted subsists through Him, and al-Ash'ari chose another expression, that the attributes of the Exalted exist through the essence of the Exalted. And others besides you from the Ash'aris mentioned all this in their books, among them Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, who mentioned in his book translated as al-Muqni' – which you skinned completely, rather you plagiarized most of it with its wording, organization, and arrangement, and attributed the authorship to yourself, and dressed it up for the weak among your followers, and fathered it with your name – he mentioned these chapters in that book with the wording you mentioned, and likewise others among the Ash'aris. If the matter is so, is this binding argument from you about the union of state and inherence anything but sluggish ignorance, manifest arbitrariness, clear impudence, and evident insolence driven by your confusion over your sect?' Then we say: 'If we had denied you the statement of the union of agent and act and you had adduced this, it would have required a kind of imagination. But since we denied you the statement of the union of act and object of act and then you busied yourself with adducing such an analogy, that was ignorance on your part of the positions of binding argument and commitment. And with Allah is protection and deliverance.'
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector attempts to draw an analogy between the union of state (hal) and inherence (hulul) on one hand, and the union of act (fi'l) and object of act (maf'ul) on the other, arguing that if the former is allowed, the latter must also be allowed. The author's answer proceeds in the objector's own order: First, he reminds the objector that in his own book on divine attributes, he argued that God's knowledge does not inhere in Him because inherence implies spatial settling, which is impossible for an attribute of God. The objector himself distinguished between inherence and subsistence, using metaphorical language for creatures but literal terms for God. Second, the author points out that the objector cited the disagreement between early attribute-theologians (Abdullah ibn Sa'id, al-Qalanisi) and al-Ash'ari, where al-Ash'ari avoided the language of inherence. Third, the author accuses the objector of plagiarizing Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub's al-Muqni', using the same wording and arrangement, but then condemning him for inconsistency. The conclusion is that the objector's argument is pure ignorance and arrogance. The author then adds a final point: even if they had denied the union of agent and act, the objection might have some plausibility, but since they specifically deny the union of act and object of act, the objector's analogy is completely irrelevant, showing his ignorance of where binding arguments apply.

Key Terms
حال (hal)
حلول (hulul)
فعل (fi'l)
مفعول (maf'ul)
حل (to settle)
توقيف (restriction to scriptural usage)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that hal (state) and hulul (inherence) are synonymous and that the union of state and inherence is a straightforward concept.
Correction: This passage shows they are distinct: inherence implies spatial settling, which cannot apply to God, so the union is not straightforward.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis of Nasafi's first refutation?
A) He cites a consensus of scholars.
B) He quotes the objector's own earlier work where the objector denied divine inherence.
C) He introduces a new definition of hal.
D) He accuses the objector of plagiarism.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's first refutation relies on the objector's own admission that divine attributes do not inhere.

Have you ever used a principle to argue something, only to realize you had denied that same principle elsewhere? How does this passage challenge you to be consistent in your statements about Allah?
?
When you speak about Allah's attributes, do you carefully consider the linguistic and theological implications—or do you casually adopt terms that might imply imperfection? What specific step will you take today to ensure your words about Allah are based on revelation?
?
Nasafi refutes his opponent by quoting the opponent's own earlier words. If your own statements about Allah were thrown back at you, would they support the truth or betray contradictions? What change in your study habits will you make to avoid theological inconsistency?

The Charge of Inconsistency and the Final Point
Nasafi completes his refutation with a plagiarism accusation and a final logical point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's further refutation: he accuses the objector of plagiarizing earlier works and then misapplying the analogy, revealing ignorance of where binding arguments apply.

Core Idea
The objector's argument is invalid because he misapplies the analogy and is intellectually dishonest.

Nasafi continues his refutation by highlighting the objector's inconsistency in citing the disagreement among attribute theologians, and then levels a serious charge of plagiarism. Finally, he points out a fundamental logical mistake: the objector applied the analogy to the wrong pair.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disagreement among attribute theologians
Read how Nasafi recalls the objector's mention of the difference between early scholars like Abdullah ibn Sa'id and al-Qalanisi who said 'subsisting through Him' and al-Ash'ari who said 'existing through His essence'.
This shows the objector himself was aware of alternative terminologies.
2
Understand the plagiarism accusation
Nasafi accuses the objector of plagiarizing the book 'al-Muqni'' by Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, taking its wording and arrangement. Note the strong language: 'skinned', 'plagiarized', 'attributed to yourself'.
This is a personal attack, but it serves to show the objector's lack of originality and possible inconsistency.
3
Grasp the final logical point
Nasafi explains that even if they had denied the union of agent (fa'il) and act (fi'l), the analogy might have some force. But they specifically deny the union of act (fi'l) and object of act (maf'ul). So the objector's analogy is misdirected, showing his ignorance.
This highlights the precise point of contention.

حال (1) بحلول هو نفسه ولم يلزم القول بأن الحلول غير الحال، فلما جاز القول باتحاد الحال والحلول جاز القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول ؟ والجواب عنه: أن يقال له، إنك أنت الذي ذكرت في مسألة الصفات في كتابك أن علم الباري لا يحله وليس هو محلا لعلمه، ولا لسائر صفات ذاته وإن كانت موجودة بذاته، لأن الحلول في اللغة هو السكون يقال حل فلان وارتحل، والعلم غير ساكن في العالم بل يستحيل أن توصف الصفة بالسكون والحركة ، ثم زعمت وكذلك الحركة غير حالة في المتحرك لما ذكرنا غير أنه يستعمل في وصف غيره ألفاظ المجاز، فأما في أوصاف الباري جل وعلا فيراعى التوقيف والحقيقة. ثم ذكرت الخلاف بين أصحاب الصفات (2) أن عبد الله بن سعيد (3) وأبا العباس القلانسي وغيرهما من متقدمي أصحاب الصفات كانوا يقولون: إن علم الباري قائم به، والأشعري اختار عبارة أخرى، وهي أن صفات الباري موجودة بذات الباري، وذكر هذا كله غيرك من الأشعريين في كتبهم منهم أبو منصور بن أيوب (4) ذكر في كتابه المترجم بالمقنع وهو الذي سلخته سلخا بل انتحلت أكثره بعبارته ونظمه وترتيبه ونسبت التصنيف إلى نفسك ولبسته على الضعفة من أصحابك وزوجته باسمك، ذكر هذه الفصول في ذلك الكتاب باللفظ الذي ذكرته، وكذا غير كما () من الأشعرية، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، هل يكون هذا الإلزام منك باتحاد الحال والحلول إلا غفلة راكدة وتحكما ظاهرا ووقاحة ظاهرة ووقاحة بينة دفعتك إليها الحيرة في نحلتك. ثم نقول : لو كنا أبينا عليك القول (2) باتحاد الفاعل والفعل فأوردت هذا لكان يوجب نوع خيال، فأما إذا كنا أبينا عليك (1) القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول ثم اشتغلت بإيراد مثل هذا النظير، كان ذلك منك جهلا بمواضع الإلزام والالتزام، وبالله العصمة والنجاة.
(Someone) said: 'If the state is the same as inherence and it is not necessary to say that inherence is other than the state, then when it is permissible to say that state and inherence are united, it becomes permissible to say that act and object of act are united?' Third, the author accuses the objector of plagiarizing Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub's al-Muqni', using the same wording and arrangement, but then condemning him for inconsistency. The conclusion is that the objector's argument is pure ignorance and arrogance. The author then adds a final point: even if they had denied the union of agent and act, the objection might have some plausibility, but since they specifically deny the union of act and object of act, the objector's analogy is completely irrelevant, showing his ignorance of where binding arguments apply.

Key Terms
قائم به (subsisting through Him)
موجود بذاته (existing through His essence)
سلخ (to skin/plagiarize)
انتحل (to plagiarize)
إلزام (binding argument)
التزام (commitment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Nasafi's refutation is purely ad hominem and lacks logical substance.
Correction: While the plagiarism charge is personal, Nasafi also provides a concrete logical distinction showing why the objector's analogy is misapplied.

Checkpoint
What is Nasafi's final logical point about the objector's analogy?
A) The objector should have used a different analogy involving agent and act.
B) The objector's analogy would work if applied to agent and act, but he used it for act and object, which is wrong.
C) The objector's analogy is correct in principle but poorly phrased.
D) The objector's analogy is invalid because he plagiarized.

Key Takeaway
The objector's analogy fails because it targets the wrong relation and he contradicts his own sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever argued against a position that you didn't fully understand, only to later realize your argument was misapplied like the objector's? What will you do the next time you feel compelled to refute a viewpoint without first learning its exact claims?
?
The objector is accused of intellectual dishonesty: plagiarizing and presenting ideas as his own. Examine your own heart—do you ever 'borrow' arguments or insights from others without true understanding or proper acknowledgment? Will you commit to deeper study and honesty in your learning today?
?
Nasafi's logical point hinges on knowing where to apply a binding argument (ilzam). Where in your own faith or life have you been applying the wrong standard or analogy, expecting it to hold? What would it cost you to admit that misapplication and realign with truth?

Exposing the Critic's Contradiction on Inherence
Nasafi exposes a critic's contradictory use of Ash'ari and Qalanisi positions on inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes a critic's inconsistency by showing the internal disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi on the inherence of accidents.

Core Idea
The critic's argument collapses when his own sources contradict each other.

This lesson presents Nasafi's response to a critic who tried to refute the Maturidi position on inherence by combining arguments from two different Ash'ari scholars. Nasafi points out that the critic has inadvertently mixed two conflicting positions, revealing a contradiction that undermines his objection. As we read, notice how Nasafi uses historical disagreements to dismantle the critic's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the gist.
This passage contains several references; don't worry about them yet.
2
Identify the sources used by the critic
The critic (mushanni') uses arguments from Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi and Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub. Look for their names and their positions on inherence.
The critic thinks he can combine them, but they contradict.
3
Understand the Ash'ari–Qalanisi disagreement
Ibn Furak narrates that al-Qalanisi allowed inherence of accidents, while al-Ash'ari did not. How does this critique the critic's use of both?
If the critic uses an argument from someone who allows inherence and another from someone who doesn't, his overall position is inconsistent.
4
Analyze Nasafi's answer and its connection to the Mu'tazila
Nasafi turns to a parallel argument from the Mu'tazila about divine attributes, asking the critic whether that argument is correct. This is a rhetorical trap to expose the critic's inconsistency.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so we don't see the critic's response.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how knowing historical disagreements between scholars is used to refute a poorly constructed argument.
This shows the importance of accuracy in citing authoritative sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا المشنع رأى الاعتراض بفصل الحال والحلول من عبد القاهر البغدادي، والقول باستحالة الحلول على الأعراض من أبي منصور بن أيوب(3)، فكان ضدا لكلا الصالحين ولم يعلم لغفلته أن بينهما تناقضا على أن ابن فورك(4) حكى خلافا بين الأشعري و القلانسي فقال : جوز القلانسي القول بأن العرض حل في الجسم، والأشعري لا يجوز ذلك، وإذا كان الأشعري لا يجوز ذلك كيف يقال الحال والحلول شيء واحد، ثم قال : هذا القائل إنما يكون الضرب غير المضروب لوجود المضروب غير مضروب، فلما صار مضروبا بعد أن لم يكن مضروبا افتقر إلى معنى لأجله كان مضروبا ، ويستحيل وجود المخلوق مع عدم الخلق، فكان هذا هو الفرق بين البابين نقل هذا بعينه عن كلام عبد القاهر . والجواب عنه () : قلنا : إنك لا تزال تأتي بكلام يوجب زيادة(6) التعجب من غفلتك، أليس أن المعتزلة يقولون في مسألة الصفات: إن العلم إنما كان في الشاهد معنى وراء العالم لوجودنا إياه مرة عالما ومرة غير عالم، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه وجوده غير عالم فلم يوجد دليل كون علمه معنى وراء العلم، واعتمد على هذا الكلام الكعبي ) وغيره في الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب، أكان ذلك صحيحا؟ فإن قال:
Then this critic saw the objection concerning the distinction between state and inherence from Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi, and the statement about the impossibility of inherence for accidents from Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, so he was contradictory to both righteous ones, and did not know due to his heedlessness that there is a contradiction between them. Moreover, Ibn Furak narrated a disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi, saying: 'Al-Qalanisi allowed the statement that an accident inheres in a body, and al-Ash'ari does not allow that. And since al-Ash'ari does not allow that, how can one say that state and inherence are one thing?' Then he said: 'This person (the speaker) only makes the beater other than the beaten due to the existence of the beaten being other than unbeaten. When it became beaten after not having been beaten, it needed a meaning by which it became beaten. And it is impossible for the created to exist without creation. So this is the difference between the two categories.' He transmitted this exactly from the speech of Abd al-Qahir. The answer to him: we said: 'You keep coming with speech that causes increase of astonishment at your heedlessness. Do not the Mu'tazila say in the issue of attributes: "Knowledge in the witnessed realm was a meaning beyond the knower because we find him sometimes knowing and sometimes not knowing, while it is impossible for Allah to exist not knowing, so no evidence is found for His knowledge being a meaning beyond knowledge"? And al-Ka'bi and others relied on this speech in the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen. Is that correct? Then if he says: ...'
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection from a critic (al-mushanni') who combined two different arguments: one from Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi about the distinction between state and inherence, and another from Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub about the impossibility of inherence for accidents. The author points out that the critic is contradicting both scholars, since there is a known disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi on whether accidents inhere in bodies: al-Qalanisi allowed it, al-Ash'ari did not. If al-Ash'ari does not allow inherence, then saying that state and inherence are one thing is problematic. The critic then transmits a physical example from Abd al-Qahir: the beater and the beaten are not identical because the beaten needs a meaning (the act of beating) to become beaten, just as the created cannot exist without creation. This is the difference between the two categories (presumably state/inherence vs act/object). The author's answer is sharp: the critic's speech is astonishingly heedless. The author redirects to the Mu'tazila's argument on divine attributes, where they claimed that knowledge in the witnessed world is a meaning beyond the knower because we observe change, but for God, change is impossible, so no evidence exists for His knowledge being a separate meaning. Al-Ka'bi relied on this. The author then asks if that argument is correct, implying that the critic is inconsistent if he accepts it. The passage cuts off as the critic's response begins.

Key Terms
مشنع (critic/objector)
عبد القاهر البغدادي (Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi)
أبو منصور بن أيوب (Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub)
ابن فورك (Ibn Furak)
الأشعري (al-Ash'ari)
القلانسي (al-Qalanisi)
عرض (accident)
حلول (inherence)
ضرب (beating)
مضروب (beaten)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
كعبي (al-Ka'bi)
شاهد (witnessed realm)
غائب (unseen realm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Ash'aris allow the inherence of accidents in bodies.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari himself denied it, while al-Qalanisi allowed it, showing an internal disagreement within the Ash'ari school.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Furak's narration, what is the difference between al-Ash'ari and al-Qalanisi on inherence?
A) Al-Ash'ari allowed accidents to inhere in bodies, al-Qalanisi did not.
B) Al-Qalanisi allowed accidents to inhere in bodies, al-Ash'ari did not.
C) Both allowed inherence of accidents.
D) Both denied inherence of accidents.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the critic's objection is self-defeating because it combines incompatible positions from different authorities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself defending a belief by quoting authorities without checking whether those authorities are consistent with each other? What would it mean for you to abandon that habit and seek coherence in your own understanding of Aqeedah?
?
Nasafi calls the critic's heedlessness 'astonishing'. In your own life, where might you be heedless about contradictions in your religious knowledge or practice? Will you investigate them now, or let them remain unchallenged?
?
The critic assumed he could combine arguments from different scholars without internal conflict. Are you willing to reconsider your cherished arguments if they are shown to be inconsistent—even if that means conceding to an opponent? What is that concession costing you?

The Three Divisions for Takwin's Origination
Al-Nasafi enumerates the three possible divisions for takwin's origination and introduces their subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible divisions for takwin's origination and the two subcategories for each division.

Core Idea
Takwin's origination must fall into one of three categories regarding its locus.

Al-Nasafi begins by establishing the logical options for takwin's origination. He argues that if takwin is not eternal, it must be originated, and then enumerates three possible loci for that origination. Each of these loci further divides into two subcases: either the takwin occurs by itself or by God's act of bringing into existence. This initial framing sets up the refutations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview. Notice the three divisions and the two subcategories.
Pay attention to the structure: premise, three main divisions, two subcategories.
2
Identify the three main divisions
List the three possibilities: (1) takwin occurs in a locus other than God's essence, (2) occurs not in a locus, (3) occurs in God's essence.
These are exhaustive categories.
3
Understand the subcategories
Each main division has two subcategories: either takwin occurs by itself (bi-nafsihi) or by God's act of bringing into existence (bi-iḥdāthillāh).
4
Preview the refutation
The passage then states that all these possibilities are impossible, setting up the refutation in the next lesson.
We will cover the refutation in lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخلو إما أن كان أزليا كما قلنا، وإما أن كان محدثا. ولا وجه لكونه محدثا لأنه ينقسم إلى أقسام ثلاثة ثم ينقسم كل قسم إلى قسمين وكلها ممتنعة، وفي القول بها ممتنعا إذ لا بد عند القول بحدوثه من إثباته على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ثم على أحد القسمين، فيكون ذلك إثبات ما قام دليل امتناعه وهو باطل.
[بيان الأقسام الثلاثة]
وبيان تلك الأقسام الأولية: أنه إما أن حدث في محل سوى ذات الله تعالى، أو حدث لا في محل، أو حدث في ذات الله تعالى وكل قسم ينقسم إلى قسمين: إما أن حدث بنفسه أو بإحداث الله تعالى ولا وجه إلى القول بأن حدث بإحداث الله لأن الكلام في التكوين الثاني كهو في الأول، فيؤدي إلى إحداث التكوين الثاني بتكوين ثالث، والثالث برابع إلى ما لا يتناهى كما هو مذهب معمر. وهو باطل، إذ القول بتعلق حدوث العالم بما لا يتناهى من الأسباب يمنع من حدوثه ووجوده، وهو موجود بالحس، محدث بالدليل الضروري فكان ذلك دليلا على بطلان هذه المقالة وهو مثل قول الدهرية لا حادث إلا وقبله حادث، وقد بينا فساد ذلك، ولي القول بصحة هذا قول بقدم العالم، والقول بقدمه يوجد، بطلان التكوين، فإذا هذا قول يوجب صحته بطلانه.
ولا وجه إلى القول بحدوثه لا بإحداث أحد، لأنه لو جاز هذا في التكوين لجاز في كل العالم وقد مر إبطال ذلك في مسألة إثبات الصانع.
It is not free from being either eternal as we said, or originated. And there is no way for it to be originated because it divides into three divisions, then each division divides into two, and all of them are impossible. And in saying they are impossible, because when saying it is originated, it is necessary to affirm it upon one of these three divisions, then upon one of the two subdivisions, so that would be affirming something whose impossibility has been proven, and that is false.
[Explanation of the three divisions]
And the explanation of those primary divisions: that either it occurred in a locus other than the essence of God Most High, or it occurred not in a locus, or it occurred in the essence of God Most High. And each division divides into two: either it occurred by itself or by an act of God's bringing-into-existence (iḥdāth). And there is no way to say that it occurred by an act of God's bringing-into-existence because the discussion concerning the second takwin (bringing into existence) is the same as concerning the first, leading to the act of bringing into existence the second takwin by a third takwin, and the third by a fourth, ad infinitum, as is the doctrine of Muʿammar. And that is false, for the claim that the temporal origination of the world depends on an infinite chain of causes prevents its temporal origination and existence, while it is existent by sense-perception and originated by necessary proof. So that was a proof of the falsehood of this doctrine, and it is like the saying of the Dahriyya: 'No event but before it is an event.' And we have already shown the corruption of that. And the claim of the correctness of this is a claim of the eternity of the world, and the claim of its eternity necessitates the falsehood of takwin. So this is a saying whose soundness necessitates its own falsehood.
And there is no way to say that it occurs [i.e., takwin is originated] without an act of bringing into existence by anyone, because if this were permissible for takwin, it would be permissible for everything in the world, and the refutation of that has already passed in the issue of proving the Maker.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the first two possible ways in which al-takwin (the act of bringing into existence) could be originated (muhdath), given the premise that it cannot be eternal. The author, al-Nasafi, first states the premise: either takwin is eternal (azalī) or originated. He then argues that if it is originated, it must fall into one of three categories: (1) occurring in a locus other than God's essence, (2) occurring not in a locus, or (3) occurring in God's essence. Each category has two subcategories: either it occurs by itself (bi-nafsihi) or by God's act of bringing into existence (bi-iḥdāthillāh). The author then refutes the subcategories for the first two main categories. The subcategory of being brought into existence by God leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul), because the same question would apply to the second takwin, and so on. This is the doctrine of Muʿammar, which is false because an infinite chain of causes would prevent the world from ever coming into existence, whereas we know it exists. This is similar to the Dahriyya's claim that every event is preceded by another event, which we have already refuted. The subcategory of being self-originated is also false, because if it were allowed for takwin, it would be allowed for everything in the world, a point already refuted in the proof of the existence of the Creator. Thus, only the third possibility remains, which will be discussed next.

Key Terms
التكوين (al-takwin)
الأزلي (al-azalī)
المحدث (al-muhdath)
الأقسام الثلاثة (the three divisions)
الإحداث (al-iḥdāth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, if takwin is originated, how many possible main divisions are there for its origination?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 6
D) 9

Key Takeaway
Takwin's origination must be one of three possibilities regarding its locus, each with two further subcases, but all are claimed to be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realize that God's attribute of bringing creation into existence (takwin) cannot be originated in any possible locus—not in His essence nor elsewhere—do you truly grasp that your very existence is sustained each moment by an eternal act of God? If He ceased to 'takwin' for an instant, you would cease to be. How does that truth transform the way you will seek His help today?
?
The author systematically eliminates every alternative except the eternity of takwin. Are there false alternatives you cling to in your life—hoping that something other than God could be self-sufficient, or that you could exist independently for even a moment? What is one subtle way you act as if you are self-created, and what concrete step can you take today to acknowledge your total dependence on God's eternal power?

Refutation of the First Two Divisions: Infinite Regress and Self-Origination
Al-Nasafi refutes the subcases of takwin being originated by God's act or by itself, showing they lead to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of takwin being originated by God's act leads to an infinite regress, and why self-origination is also impossible.

Core Idea
If takwin is originated by God's act, an infinite regress results; if self-originated, it applies to everything, which is absurd.

In this lesson, al-Nasafi refutes the first two main divisions of takwin's origination. He argues against the subcase where takwin is brought into existence by God, showing that it leads to an infinite chain of takwins, making the world's origination impossible. He also refutes the subcase of self-origination, pointing out that if allowed for takwin, it would be allowed for everything, which contradicts the proof of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the arguments against the two subcases.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If takwin1 is originated by God's iḥdāth, then the iḥdāth itself is a takwin (takwin2). The same question applies to takwin2, leading to an infinite chain. This is the doctrine of Muammar.
This argument shows that an infinite regress prevents the world's coming into existence.
3
Analyze the self-origination argument
If takwin could originate by itself without any act, then everything in the world could also be self-originated. But this is already refuted in the proof of the Creator.
4
Connect to the Dahriyya
The infinite regress argument is similar to the Dahriyya's claim that every event is preceded by another, which has been refuted earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if takwin is originated by God's act, there is no problem because God can create as many takwins as He wishes.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that this leads to an infinite regress which makes the world's origination impossible, because an infinite chain of causes has no beginning.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if takwin is originated by God's act (bi-iḥdāthillāh) according to al-Nasafi?
A) It would be eternal
B) It would lead to an infinite regress
C) It would be self-originated
D) It would occur in God's essence

Key Takeaway
The possibility of takwin being originated by God's act leads to an absurd infinite regress, and self-origination is also impossible; thus only the third division remains to be examined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made excuses for a sin by thinking, 'I'll deal with it after this one more time'? This lesson shows that an infinite chain of excuses prevents real change, just as an infinite chain of takwins prevents existence. Is there an area where you are using 'one more step' to avoid confronting yourself? What will you do to break that chain today?
?
The lesson argues that if takwin were self-originated, everything could be self-originated, making God unnecessary. In what part of your life are you living as if you are self-sufficient—relying on your own strength rather than God's origination of your actions? How would acknowledging God as the origin of your good deeds change your attitude?
?
When you read that an infinite regress of causes would prevent the world from existing, it highlights that beginnings require a First Cause. In your spiritual journey, have you been waiting for a 'perfect moment' that never comes because you imagine an endless series of conditions? What is one step you can take right now, trusting that the First Cause is already at work in you?

The First Proof: Takwin as an Accident Requires a Locus
Nasafi presents the first proof and refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that takwin, as an accident, cannot exist without a locus, and how the objection of Ibn al-Rawandi is refuted.

Core Idea
Takwin is an accident; accidents cannot subsist alone.

Imam Nasafi begins by stating that the claim of takwin occurring not in a locus is false. He then presents his first proof: takwin is a contingent event and thus an accident, and accidents by nature cannot exist independently. He anticipates and refutes the objection of Ibn al-Rawandi, who denies that takwin is an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the translation.
Notice the two-part structure: presentation of the proof and refutation of the objection.
2
Identify the core claim
The claim is that takwin cannot occur without a locus. The author proves this by stating that takwin is an accident.
3
Understand the analogy
The author compares takwin to other accidents like blackness and whiteness. All accidents require a locus.
This is a key analogy in kalam.
4
Follow the refutation
Ibn al-Rawandi objects that takwin is not an accident because it does not inhere. The author uses a dilemma to refute him.
The dilemma forces a choice between absurdity and consistency.
5
Conclude the first proof
The refutation establishes that takwin is indeed an accident, so it must occur in a locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدوث التكوين لا في محل باطل لوجهين. ثم نقول : كل قسم من ذلك باطل في نفسه أيضا. أما حدوثه لا في محل ففاسد من وجهين أحدهما أن التكوين الحادث عرض ووجود العرض قائما بنفسه محال كما في سائر الأعراض، كالسواد والبياض والحركة والسكون وغير ذلك من الأعراض ولن يستقيم قول صاحب هذه المقالة وهو ابن الراوندي أن التكوين ليس بعرض لأن العرض ما يعرض الجوهر فيوجد فيه، وهذا لا يعرض الجوهر بل يوجد لا في محل، والعرض هو الذي يستحيل قيامه بنفسه لا ما ليس عرض. لأنا نقول له: جواز قيام السواد والبياض والحركة والسكون وغير ذلك لا في محل، ولا تسميها أعراضا لأنها لم تعرض الجوهر؟ فإن جوز ذلك خرج عن المعقول والتحق بأهل العناد وإن لم يجوز بطل سعيه. ثم نقول : إن استحالة قيام هذه الأعراض بأنفسها ما كانت لأنها أعراض تعرض الجوهر، بل اعتراضها الجوهر ووجودها فيه لا غير، كان لاستحالة قيامها بذواتها فكانت مفتقرة إلى محل في أنفسها لما أنها صفات تتجلى بها محالها، وتمتاز بقيامها بها عما لا قيام لها بها، فلا يتصور وجودها لا على هذا السبيل.
The occurrence of creation not in a locus is false for two reasons. Then we say: Every division of that is false in itself as well. As for its occurrence not in a locus, it is invalid from two aspects. One of them is that the contingent creation (takwin) is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and the existence of an accident subsisting by itself is impossible, as is the case with other accidents, like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and other accidents. And the statement of the proponent of this article, namely Ibn al-Rawandi, that creation is not an accident because an accident is what inheres in a substance and thus exists in it, while this (creation) does not inhere in a substance but exists not in a locus, and an accident is that which is impossible for it to subsist by itself, not that which is not an accident, will not stand. For we say to him: Is it permissible for blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and other things to subsist not in a locus, and do you not call them accidents because they do not inhere in a substance? If he permits that, he departs from reason and joins the people of obstinacy; and if he does not permit it, his effort is nullified. Then we say: The impossibility of these accidents subsisting by themselves is not because they are accidents that inhere in substances; rather, their inherence in substances and their existence in them only is due to the impossibility of their subsisting by themselves. So they are in need of a locus in themselves because they are attributes by which their loci are characterized, and they are distinguished by their subsistence in them from what does not have subsistence in them. Thus their existence is not conceivable otherwise than in this way.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is a proof that takwin (the act of creation) cannot occur without a locus (maḥall). The author begins by stating that the view that takwin occurs not in a locus is false for two reasons. He then presents the first reason: takwin, being a contingent (ḥādith) event, is an accident (ʿaraḍ). Accidents cannot exist independently; they require a substance (jawhar) in which to inhere, just like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, etc. The author then refutes the objection of Ibn al-Rawandi, who claims that takwin is not an accident because an accident is defined as what inheres in a substance, whereas takwin does not inhere. The refutation uses a dilemma: if Ibn al-Rawandi allows that blackness, whiteness, etc., could exist without a locus, then he abandons reason and becomes obstinate; if he does not allow it, then his claim that takwin is not an accident fails because it shares the same property of needing a locus. The second reason is a deeper explanation: the impossibility of accidents subsisting by themselves is not merely because they inhere in substances; rather, their inherence is a consequence of their intrinsic need for a locus. They are attributes (ṣifāt) that characterize their loci, and they are distinguished by their subsistence in them. Therefore, it is inconceivable for them to exist otherwise. Thus, takwin must occur in a locus.

Key Terms
تكوين
عرض
محل
جوهر
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that takwin is not an accident because it does not inhere in a substance, so it can exist without a locus.
Correction: Imam Nasafi refutes this by showing that accidents like blackness also do not inhere in a substance in the sense of being acquired by it, yet they still require a locus to subsist. The definition of an accident is not about inherence but about dependence on a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in the passage, why does Imam Nasafi argue that takwin cannot occur without a locus?
A) Because takwin is a substance.
B) Because takwin is an accident like blackness.
C) Because Ibn al-Rawandi says so.
D) Because the Quran says so.

Key Takeaway
Takwin, as a contingent accident, cannot exist without a locus, just like any other accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when honestly examined, contradicted clear rational proof? What would it take for you to abandon that belief and follow the evidence?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi was a scholar, yet he fell into error. When you defend your theological positions, do you check your heart for pride that might prefer being 'right' over being truthful? How can you ensure you are not joining 'the people of obstinacy'?
?
The refutation here uses a dilemma: either accept a absurd consequence or concede the argument. Think of a personal conviction you hold. If you were forced to choose between two uncomfortable options, would you have the courage to follow reason wherever it leads, even if it challenges your comfort?

The Second Proof: Accidents Intrinsically Need a Locus
Nasafi explains the intrinsic need of accidents for a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that accidents need a locus not because they are accidents but because of their intrinsic nature as attributes.

Core Idea
Accidents need loci by their very essence, not by definition.

After refuting the objection, Nasafi provides a deeper explanation: the impossibility of accidents subsisting by themselves is not merely because they are accidents that inhere in substances, but because they are inherently dependent on loci as attributes that characterize their hosts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a single continuous argument.
Notice the causal language: 'ما كانت لأن... بل...'.
2
Identify the key shift
Nasafi argues that the need for a locus is not due to the definition of accident, but due to the nature of accidents as attributes.
3
Understand the attribute argument
Accidents are attributes that characterize their loci. They are distinguished by their subsistence in a locus.
This is a metaphysical point.
4
Apply to takwin
Since takwin is an accident, it falls under this principle and must occur in a locus.

Source Text

Abu Bakr's Advice and the Ten Questions
Praise of Abu Bakr, his advice, and the ten questions distinguishing Sunnis from Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of Abu Bakr's final injunction to the people of Samarkand and how the ten 'Iyadi questions serve as the core points of contention between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's final advice: cling to Sunnah, shun Mu'tazilism; the ten questions define the Sunni-Mu'tazili debate.

This brief but weighty passage from a Maturidi creedal work records the deathbed advice of a prominent scholar, Abu Bakr, to the people of Samarkand. He urges them to remain steadfast upon the path of Ahl al-Sunna and to avoid innovations, especially Mu'tazilism. The passage then introduces a set of ten questions (al-masa'il al-ashr) that form the backbone of theological disagreements between Sunnis and Mu'tazila, serving as a ready reference for students of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the dense but precise language typical of classical kalam works.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'المسائل العشر' — it may indicate a scribal duplication, but it emphasizes the importance of these questions.
2
Identify Abu Bakr's command
Locate the directive Abu Bakr gave to the people of Samarkand: to adhere to madhhab Ahl al-Sunna and avoid desires (ahwa') and innovations (bida'), specifically Mu'tazila.
This highlights the urgent need to preserve orthodox belief against emerging sects.
3
Understand the ten questions
The passage states that the ten questions (al-masa'il al-ashr) are the principles (usul) of the contentious issues between Sunnis and Mu'tazila. They are called 'Iyadi questions, likely after a scholar named 'Iyad.
A common misconception is that these are the same as the five usul of the Mu'tazila; in fact, they are a distinct set specific to the Maturidi-Mu'tazili polemic.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why a dying scholar would emphasize these points. The ten questions summarize the key differences that every Sunni student should know to defend orthodoxy.
This lesson sets the stage for studying those ten questions in detail in subsequent lessons.

Source Text

The passage then identifies a set of ten foundational questions (al-masa'il al-ashr) that constitute the core points of theological dispute between the Sunnis and the Mu'tazila, known as the 'Iyadi questions after someone named 'Iyad. This highlights that these ten questions are the basis of doctrinal differences and serve as a reference for polemics.
